  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_80322a03-0645-4c79-8415-e191016dab48'
      });
    }

      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<span id="freeTextContainer8983555800901384058">A tale of chivalry and doomed, transcendent love, <i>The Romance of Tristan and Iseult </i>is one of the most resonant works of Western literature, as well as the basis for our enduring idea of romance.   <span id="freeText8983555800901384058" style="display:none">A tale of chivalry and doomed, transcendent love, <i>The Romance of Tristan and Iseult </i>is one of the most resonant works of Western literature, as well as the basis for our enduring idea of romance. The story of the Cornish knight and the Irish princess who meet by deception, fall in love by magic, and pursue that love in defiance of heavenly and earthly law has inspired artists from Matthew Arnold to Richard Wagner. But nowhere has it been retold with greater eloquence and dignity than in Joseph BÃ©dierâs edition, which weaves several medieval sources into a seamless whole, elegantly translated by Hilaire Belloc and Paul Rosenfeld.</span>

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/1100055-i-would-be-grateful-for-opinions-on-the">I would be grateful for opinions on the best version of Tristan and Iseult to read?</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_1100055'><a id="like_count_community_question_1100055" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/1100055?resource_type=CommunityQuestion"><span class="likesCount">3 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F587359.The_Romance_of_Tristan_and_Iseult">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>
<a href="/questions/1100055-i-would-be-grateful-for-opinions-on-the">3 years ago</a>
</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>
<a href="/questions/1100055-i-would-be-grateful-for-opinions-on-the">Add your answer</a>
</li>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/15050951-shrimp"><img alt="" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_100x100-c7bfe730633c9983e27caaa66a8e244a.png" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/15050951-shrimp">Shrimp</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<span class="expandableContainer"><span class="truncatedContent">Well there's 4 people to pick from:<br/><br/>Gottfried - Considered the best version but it's almost 400 pages long and it tends to really drag<br/>Beroul - The 'dir<a class="jsShow showAction" href="#">â¦more</a></span><span class="fullContent">Well there's 4 people to pick from:<br /><br />Gottfried - Considered the best version but it's almost 400 pages long and it tends to really drag<br />Beroul - The 'dirty' version, in that it isn't beautiful poetry, and the characters are not properly defined. There's also no finished version and the translator has to finish the story by drawing from various sources.<br />Bedier - Most popular contemporary version of the legend, but considered to fall pale in comparison to both Gottfried and Beroul.<br />Oberge- Not very true to the original and not a popular version.<br /><br />When it comes down to it, it's either Gottfried or Beroul, I personally prefer Beroul because it gets the job done without too much sidetracking, and I used the internet whenever I felt the Beroul version was lacking in story.<br />Beroul is a quick and easy read, Gottfriend tends to sidetrack a lot such as in the part where he goes off track to talk about the history of Tristan.<a class="jsHide hideAction" href="#">(less)</a></span></span>
</span>
</div>
</div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer12612558777548256292">A classic myth, but maybe not the best version.<br/><br/>Tristan and Iseult is a big deal: itâs said to be a major influence for the romance between Lancelot and Guinevere, Romeo and Juliet, and pretty much the majority romantic love stories after the 12th century. This myth was passed down verbally and has been committed to text by several different authors. BÃ©dier argues that his version is adapted from the original poem, which may or may not be historically accurate. The style is conversational as if a</span>
  <span id="freeText12612558777548256292" style="display:none">A classic myth, but maybe not the best version.<br /><br />Tristan and Iseult is a big deal: itâs said to be a major influence for the romance between Lancelot and Guinevere, Romeo and Juliet, and pretty much the majority romantic love stories after the 12th century. This myth was passed down verbally and has been committed to text by several different authors. BÃ©dier argues that his version is adapted from the original poem, which may or may not be historically accurate. The style is conversational as if a bard is telling it aloud, which is probably an attempt to retell the story as closely as possible to the Cornish poem BÃ©dier argued was the original. No matter the accuracy, the story as he writes it is interesting and tragic. It would have been more interesting and tragic if it didnât start by telling you what is going to happen, but for some reason classics donât believe in the importance of the element of surprise.<br /><br />One thing that this book got me thinking about was persuasion of the reader through perspective. As long as a book clearly âsidesâ with a character as a good guy, the reader generally accepts that the character is someone they should root for. But these people were awful. If we set aside the âforbidden loveâ /affair moral conundrum, thereâs still the fact that Tristan and Iseult go around killing or almost killing several people just to keep their boot knocking secret. Nobel and fair hearted? Not so much. But I bet most people donât even notice T&amp;I are a-holes, because the narrator keeps talking about how awesome and doomed they are.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer6335543700220988919"><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1401989127i/9878383.jpg' width='400' height='267' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>Okay, so I did NOT <b>love</b> it... but it was good :)<br/><br/>I <b>LOVE</b> the 2006 film <i>Tristan & Isolde</i> for reasons.... mostly this: <br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1401900210i/9865607._SX540_.jpg' width='300' height='200' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>and this: <br/><br/><img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1395518769i/9009594._SX540_.jpg' width='256' height='144' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/><br/><br/>but also because it has one of the most poignantly beautiful forgiveness scenes I have ever seen. Rufus Sewell (as King Mark) delivers an unforgettable performance. Which is saying something because I am a HUGE James Franco fan (he is the reason I watched the movie) and Rufus Sewell stole that movie from him, hands *snicker* down!!<br/><br/>However, this translation/compilation of <i>T</i></span>
  <span id="freeText6335543700220988919" style="display:none"><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1401989127i/9878383.jpg" width="400" height="267" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>Okay, so I did NOT <b>love</b> it... but it was good :)<br><br>I <b>LOVE</b> the 2006 film <i>Tristan &amp; Isolde</i> for reasons.... mostly this: <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1401900210i/9865607._SX540_.jpg" width="300" height="200" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>and this: <br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1395518769i/9009594._SX540_.jpg" width="256" height="144" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br>but also because it has one of the most poignantly beautiful forgiveness scenes I have ever seen. Rufus Sewell (as King Mark) delivers an unforgettable performance. Which is saying something because I am a HUGE James Franco fan (he is the reason I watched the movie) and Rufus Sewell stole that movie from him, hands *snicker* down!!<br><br>However, this translation/compilation of <i>Tristan &amp; Iseult</i> didn't ravish me the way the movie did. It was good, cute and tragic in turns but I found the fantastical elements made it a lot harder to relate to. <br><br>There are big variances between the movie and book, a key one being the love potion (aka spiced wine)'s presence. I didn't like that part as it removed the humanity of the affair and, in a way, made the lovers actions more forgivable. <br><br>All and all it was quite good but I wish I'd read the book first and seen the movie second. I never thought I would say this... the movie was better.... *facepalm* I have to go hide now. <br></span>

  <span id="freeText16589743424368214210" style="display:none">His parents were wed in a castle standing above the sea called Tintagel "well fenced against all assault or engines of war, (with) its keep, which the giants had built long ago, (a) compact of great stones, like a chess board of vert and azure." He was lost for a long time, his royal origin hidden and unknown, but fate was kind and he was brought back to King Mark, ruler of Cornwall, brother of his deceased mother Blanchefleur (the husband of his late father, Rivalen King of Lyonesse). He served King Mark loyally, loving him as his lord and like a father. But there were battles fought, a dragon slain, a bird which came to King Mark with a girl's fine strand of hair and a love potion drank by the wrong couple until it all came to this: he, Tristan and Iseult the Fair love each other but Iseult the Fair is to marry King Mark. A touching scene between the two before the wedding:<br /><br />"Two days she (Iseult's lady-in-waiting) watched them, seeing them refuse all food or comfort and seeking each other as blind men seek, wretched apart and together more wretched still, for then they trembled each for the first avowal.<br />"On the third day, as Tristan neared the tent on deck where Iseult sat, she saw him coming and she said to him, very humbly, 'Come in, my lord.'<br />"'Queen,' said Tristan, 'why do you call me lord? Am I not your liege and vassal, to revere and serve and cherish you as my lady and Queen?'<br />"But Iseult answered, 'No, you know that you are my lord and my master, and I your slave. Ah, why did I not sharpen those wounds of the wounded singer, or let die that dragon-slayer in the grasses of the marsh? Why did I not, while he lay helpless in the bath, plant on him the blow of the sword I brandished? But then I did not know what now I know!'<br />"'And what is it that you know, Iseult? What is it that torments you?'<br />"'Ah, all that I know torments me, and all that I see. This sky and this sea torment me, and my body and my life.'<br />"She laid her arm upon Tristan's shoulder, the light of her eyes was drowned and her lips trembled.<br />"He repeated: 'Friend, what is it that torments you?'<br />"'THE LOVE OF YOU,' she said. Whereat he put his lips to hers."<br /><br />Aaaaaw, the collective sigh of teenage girls in an imaginary moviehouse as Tristan and Iseult exchange bodily fluids for the first time. On the other hand I, jaded by love I no longer need to suffer vicariously, just continue munching my popcorn (barbecue flavor keeps me awake) and just wait for this scene where chess is used as a signal for Iseult to cheat on her husband-king:<br /><br />"Dinas accordingly returned to Tintagel, climbed the stair and entered the hall. Under the canopy King Mark and Iseult the Fair sat over a game of chess. Dinas seated himself on a stool beside the Queen, as though to observe her play, and twice, pretending to point out moves to her, he posed his hand on the chess board: the second time, Iseult perceived on one of his fingers the jasper ring. Great joy immediately overwhelmed her. Lightly she jarred Dinas' arm, so that several pawns fell in a heap.<br />"'Look, seneschal,' said she, 'you have disturbed my game, and in a way that prevents my resuming it.'<br />"Mark left the hall, Iseult repaired to her chamber and had the seneschal called to her:<br />"'Friend, you bear a message from Tristan?"<br /><br />Wonderful, very old(circa 1300's) love story. And who was the author? There were several:<br /><br />"The good singers of old time, Beroul and Thomas of Built, Gilbert and Gottfried told this tale for lovers and none other, and, by my pen, they beg you for your prayers. They greet those who are cast down, and those in heart, those troubled and those filled with desire, those who are overjoyed and those disconsolate, all lovers. May all herein find strength against inconstancy, against unfairness and despite and loss and pain and all the bitterness of loving."<br /><br />Amen.</span>

    <a title="David Wright" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/67364883-david-wright"><img alt="David Wright" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1493516156p2/67364883.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2253912860?book_show_action=true">Jan 14, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="David Wright" class="user" itemprop="url" name="David Wright" href="/user/show/67364883-david-wright">David Wright</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2253912860" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer9976797116260438659">I absolutely love this story. As usual, I won't mention any plot specifics as such. I meant to read this a long time ago after reading Beowulf but got sidetracked. This is such a tragic tale that you will either love completely or take issue with, depending on your interpretation of what is just and right within the storyline. There are battles of mythical beasts as in Beowulf but also intrigues, jealousy, spite and heartbreak. In my opinion, the events surrounding vanquishing of certain charact</span>
  <span id="freeText9976797116260438659" style="display:none">I absolutely love this story. As usual, I won't mention any plot specifics as such. I meant to read this a long time ago after reading Beowulf but got sidetracked. This is such a tragic tale that you will either love completely or take issue with, depending on your interpretation of what is just and right within the storyline. There are battles of mythical beasts as in Beowulf but also intrigues, jealousy, spite and heartbreak. In my opinion, the events surrounding vanquishing of certain characters are totally deserved, they were cowardly and two faced backstabbers who had no loyalty whatsoever. I also felt total empathy for Tristan and Iseult, being thrown together by external events. The fact that this book has reviewers disputing viewpoints just proves the impact that this story makes, everyone will react to it in a certain way. For me, this was a work of art.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9976797116260438659" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_3387586731" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3387586731" />
    <a title="John Mccullough" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13417829-john-mccullough"><img alt="John Mccullough" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1349487620p2/13417829.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3387586731?book_show_action=true">Jun 14, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John Mccullough" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John Mccullough" href="/user/show/13417829-john-mccullough">John Mccullough</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3387586731" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12725518946367058490">Tristan and Iseult is a Medieval story of two star-crossed lovers. The King Tristan of Lyonesse, nephew of Mark, King of Cornwall at Tintagel and contemporary of King Arthur, slays an Irish champion and thus wins a wife for King Mark. The wife is Iseult the Fair, princess from Ireland. Tristan is sent to Ireland to claim Iseult and bring her back to Tintagel. Iseultâs maid concocts a love philtre that would bind Iseult and Mark to eternal love when drunk from the same cup. On the trip back to Co</span>
  <span id="freeText12725518946367058490" style="display:none">Tristan and Iseult is a Medieval story of two star-crossed lovers. The King Tristan of Lyonesse, nephew of Mark, King of Cornwall at Tintagel and contemporary of King Arthur, slays an Irish champion and thus wins a wife for King Mark. The wife is Iseult the Fair, princess from Ireland. Tristan is sent to Ireland to claim Iseult and bring her back to Tintagel. Iseultâs maid concocts a love philtre that would bind Iseult and Mark to eternal love when drunk from the same cup. On the trip back to Cornwall, Tristan and Iseult inadvertently drink the philtre instead, uniting them in an undying love. The story thereafter revolves around the difficulty of loving while married to someone else, as Mark and Iseult become husband and wife, and court values meant that they could never consummate their love while Iseult was married. And what would happen if Tristan also married?<br /><br />Bedier pieced the story together from different versions of the story, including several independent (âshortâ) stories that were not part of a larger text. Scholars seem to divide story sources into two different traditions â the courtly tradition, and a common tradition which is less courtly and perhaps older and was favored by Bedier. Written versions popped up in different locations in the late 12th century in England, Ireland and Continental Europe, but there are versions of the story as far away as Persia almost at the same time. As an anthropologist I will bet the story is older â much older â perhaps as old as the Bronze Age which in Europe is coincident with the spread of Indo-European languages and their diversification. And I would not be surprised to hear the story is even older than that. What also seems certain is that the story struck a real chord with Medieval European cultures at about the same time â the middle 1100âs.<br /><br />The story is basic â a love triangle which collides with the basic tenets of feudalism and chivalry â courtly love and loyalty to loved one and the liege relationship. Results of the relationship probably depend on cultural expectations. In the case of Bedierâs version of Tristan and Iseult it resulted in not consummating their love to honor marriage vows and Tristanâs feudal obligations to King Mark. While Bedierâs compilation is thought to favor the common origin, his reconstruction seems from my peasantâs perspective to be very courtly.<br /><br />I first ran into the story as an early teenager, reading âThe Enchanted Cup,â a novelization of the story by Dorothy James Roberts. Now as an adult it was high time to read something closer to the original(s).</span>
  <a data-text-id="12725518946367058490" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer18353042413722609873"><b>Want a 12th-century romance with a mixture of:<br/><br/>Adventures + tragedy + some magic potions + a lot of quests + that weird concept of honor = an outstanding romance that inspired a lot of work that came after it.</b><br/>Bedier is a great storyteller too. <br/><br/>Since my mother grew up reading medieval novels about King Arthur and his knights of the round table I always had a lot of books on that topic and it takes me to that nostalgic time when she would tell me about it. <br/>This type of story takes me to a whole dif</span>
  <span id="freeText18353042413722609873" style="display:none"><b>Want a 12th-century romance with a mixture of:<br /><br />Adventures + tragedy + some magic potions + a lot of quests + that weird concept of honor = an outstanding romance that inspired a lot of work that came after it.</b><br />Bedier is a great storyteller too. <br /><br />Since my mother grew up reading medieval novels about King Arthur and his knights of the round table I always had a lot of books on that topic and it takes me to that nostalgic time when she would tell me about it. <br />This type of story takes me to a whole different time and place and I really enjoy it myself. Nothing more to say here, besides a very subjective 5/5.<br />------------------------------------------------------------------<br />12th-century romance with adventures, a magic potion, and a tragic ending? Sign me up. Review to come.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer8544038459673383510">Lackluster is the only word I can think of to describe this book. Tristan and Iseult is supposed to be a passionate romance, but I wasn't feeling it here at all. In fact, I cared more about Tristan and Mark's relationship than about either man's relationship with Iseult. As for Iseult, I liked her a lot when she wanted to stab Tristan to avenge her kinsman, but then she lost all personality for the rest of the book.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText1659462484897275157" style="display:none">I adore this story, and I know that the beautiful writing of Joseph Bedier is largely to thank- but I must give credit to the specific reader at librivox who helped me to fall in love with the tale.<br /><br />I listened to Tristan and Iseult as an audiobook, acquired through Librivox and read by Joy Chan. This absolutely ranks as one of my favorite books of all time, and I would recommend it to anyone. (Just a note- the movie did NOT do it justice!)</span>

Although this story line has been used endlessly later, in many classic romance stories like the Arthurian romance of Lancelot and Guinevere, this is a very good read and the original.<br/><br/>This one, Bedier's translation which was then translated by Hilaire Belloc) is the living piece of mythology.   <span id="freeText14578620858570482417" style="display:none">Searched and read the e-book. Why? Tristan and Isolde was one of the most influential romances in the medieval period. Although this story line has been used endlessly later, in many classic romance stories like the Arthurian romance of Lancelot and Guinevere, this is a very good read and the original.<br /><br />This one, Bedier's translation which was then translated by Hilaire Belloc) is the living piece of mythology. He tells Tristan and Isolde the same way you've probably heard it a thousand times. I believe, how this tale was meant to be told. (This is more close to Beroul's poem.)<br />This has everything: dragons, magic, love philtres, cunning dwarves, unethical opponents, lepers and hermits, castles, forests, sea, revenge, murder, deciet, love and loyalty. <br /><br />If you are at all interested in mythology, especially that of Arthurian theme or Medieval literature, Bedier's translation of "Tristan and Iseult" is the one for you. You won't be disappointed. </span>

    <a title="Jess" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2249200-jess"><img alt="Jess" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1278553873p2/2249200.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jess" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jess" href="/user/show/2249200-jess">Jess</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer70621612&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer70621612" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5963830481957396934"><p>An interesting tale, but I did not care for the love chemistry involved. Tristan and Iseult fall in love because they drink a love potion together. They have no choice but to love each other. All their sin throughout the book is pardoned because it's not their "fault."</p><p>It is interesting, though, to note the choices they do make. Though they are in love, they are still in possession of their minds, able to think. An interesting contrast of the mind against the body. I see a relation to our common </p></span>
  <span id="freeText5963830481957396934" style="display:none"><p>An interesting tale, but I did not care for the love chemistry involved. Tristan and Iseult fall in love because they drink a love potion together. They have no choice but to love each other. All their sin throughout the book is pardoned because it's not their "fault."</p><p>It is interesting, though, to note the choices they do make. Though they are in love, they are still in possession of their minds, able to think. An interesting contrast of the mind against the body. I see a relation to our common ideas of love; the potion represents the love we fall into; their choices to honor King Mark, their friends, and each other represents a love we choose, a love we have because we care about others.</p></span>
  <a data-text-id="5963830481957396934" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1524219159" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1524219159" />
    <a title="Paloma Meir" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/36085050-paloma-meir"><img alt="Paloma Meir" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1554746664p2/36085050.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1524219159?book_show_action=true">Jan 24, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paloma Meir" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paloma Meir" href="/user/show/36085050-paloma-meir">Paloma Meir</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1524219159" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7636759614021623593">Most of my reading life has been spent searching for a story that will make me feel as I felt when first reading Wuthering Heights. I feel your pain, Tristan and Iseult. I've read so many retellings of this story, but never the source. Happy to have finally read it.<br/><br/>*** John Updike's Brazil is my favorite reimag</span>
  <span id="freeText7636759614021623593" style="display:none">Most of my reading life has been spent searching for a story that will make me feel as I felt when first reading Wuthering Heights. The Romance of Tristan and Iseult didn't succeed (and nothing else has either), but I loved it anyway. <br /><br />"Apart the lovers could neither live nor die, for it was life and death together..."   <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2282678358" />
    <a title="Johanna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/61148789-johanna"><img alt="Johanna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596978862p2/61148789.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2282678358?book_show_action=true">Feb 04, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Johanna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Johanna" href="/user/show/61148789-johanna">Johanna</a>
      </span>

          added it


          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780486440194" href="/book/show/24669.The_Romance_of_Tristan_and_Iseult">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2282678358" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1560843044679468358">In this retelling (I understand there are many versions of this tale), the starting point of Tristan and Iseult's love was the accident in which they drank a love potion meant for Iseult to drink with King Mark. The effect is lasting, and from this all mishaps happen.<br/>Perhaps it was because of this idea of a love out of potion, it becomes rather hard not to consider all the remaining incidents as mere exaggeration.<br/><br/>The book is presented as if a tale is told to us (as 'lords', his audience), and I </span>
  <span id="freeText1560843044679468358" style="display:none">In this retelling (I understand there are many versions of this tale), the starting point of Tristan and Iseult's love was the accident in which they drank a love potion meant for Iseult to drink with King Mark. The effect is lasting, and from this all mishaps happen.<br />Perhaps it was because of this idea of a love out of potion, it becomes rather hard not to consider all the remaining incidents as mere exaggeration.<br /><br />The book is presented as if a tale is told to us (as 'lords', his audience), and I had at times expected there be a breach in the narration for the storyteller to address the invisible audience ("Why doesn't she ask the Queen for an antidote, you ask?"), or any pauses to relay the reason behind the protagonists' acts, or even some nods to tell us "for what reason, none knows." Then again, it doesnt seem that this is meant to be anything but a straightforward retelling of Tristan and Iseult.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1560843044679468358" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText4568887081216528741" style="display:none">I suppose the novel has become out of date, for I couldnât bring myself to enjoy it as much as I probably should have. Both the plot and the writing turned out to be just average, besides I find Tristan and Iseult to be very hypocritical. In my opinion, it made God look bad by praising their affair and claiming it was right to murder their antagonists.   function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('HGqYpyqeWT6PVvBDReegWuugwTMW3zjwjEQ8gJWICFLrfUKDMmJgvnc/ZNIt0neBmvf1N+9f7zjHkQV4FbE4Tw==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('IfYnOIzmF6Yw10hPIqPbRUEbJSjbwpt6K/5/6b/v9NPW4f0clBouJsi+3N5KlgyeMEwRLCJCTLJgK0YRP9bEzg==')})
  }
//]]>

  <span id="freeText9422073307172407680" style="display:none">BÃ©dier was born in Paris, France to Adolphe BÃ©dier, a lawyer of Breton origin, and spent his childhood in RÃ©union. He was a professor of medieval French literature at the UniversitÃ© de Fribourg, Switzerland (1889â1891) and the CollÃ¨ge de France, Paris (c. 1893).<br /><br />Modern theories of the fabliaux and the chansons de geste are based on two of BÃ©dier's studies.<br /><br />BÃ©dier revived interest in several important old French texts, including Le roman de Tristan et Iseut (1900), La chanson de Roland (1921), and Les fabliaux (1893). He was a member of the AcadÃ©mie franÃ§aise from 1920 until his death.<br /><br />His Tristan et Iseut was translated into Cornish by A. S. D. Smith, into English by Hilaire Belloc and Paul Rosenfeld, and into German by Rudolf G. Binding.<br /><br />BÃ©dier was also joint editor of the two-volume LittÃ©rature franÃ§aise, one of the most valuable modern general histories of French literature. He was elected a Foreign Honorary Member of the American Academy of Arts and Sciences in 1929.<br /><br />BÃ©dier died in Le Grand-Serre, France</span>
